    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<span id="freeTextContainer7542098231891136288"> <strong>“Thurber is...a landmark in American humor...he is the funniest artist who ever lived.” — <em>New Republic</em></strong><br/><br/>Widely hailed as one of the finest humorist of the twentieth century, James Thurber looks back at his own life growing up in Columbus, Ohio, with the same humor and sharp wit that defined his famous sketches and writings. In <em>My Life and Hard times,</em> first published in 1933</span>
  <span id="freeText7542098231891136288" style="display:none"> <strong>“Thurber is...a landmark in American humor...he is the funniest artist who ever lived.” — <em>New Republic</em></strong><br /><br />Widely hailed as one of the finest humorist of the twentieth century, James Thurber looks back at his own life growing up in Columbus, Ohio, with the same humor and sharp wit that defined his famous sketches and writings. In <em>My Life and Hard times,</em> first published in 1933, he recounts the delightful chaos and frustrations of family, boyhood, youth, odd dogs, recalcitrant machinery, and the foibles of human nature.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2745202182" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1036875820006284201">The fact that this 1946 Bantam book is falling apart as I read and laugh at the cracked pages on a dark, rainy Sunday when all the world’s news makes me sick and my dog doesn’t want to walk, let alone poop, on New York City’s dismal wet streets seems somehow fitting.<br/><br/>With a dedication to Andy White for instigating the publication of these iconic American cartoons, a reverential preface by Dorothy Parker, and Thurber’s essential humor and pathos, what’s not to like?<br/></span>

          </span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=humor">humor</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer89740762" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6485365497806575250">Before starting: I need something light, and I love memoirs, so this should be perfect.<br/><br/>And it was! A very short memoir that says it all. Some writers don't have to talk and talk and talk, just a few short episodes, all humorous, tell about the essential elements of James Thurber's boyhood growing up in Columbus, Ohio - the flatlands. Even though the chapters could be seen as short stories, they are not! They are true episodes in this humorist's life. The book was written in the 30s and it has lo</span>
  <span id="freeText6485365497806575250" style="display:none">Before starting: I need something light, and I love memoirs, so this should be perfect.<br /><br />And it was! A very short memoir that says it all. Some writers don't have to talk and talk and talk, just a few short episodes, all humorous, tell about the essential elements of James Thurber's boyhood growing up in Columbus, Ohio - the flatlands. Even though the chapters could be seen as short stories, they are not! They are true episodes in this humorist's life. The book was written in the 30s and it has lost none of its charm and humor. That is what I call a classic.So many classic's are dated in their writting style. This isn't. What is covered: his relationship with his mother, from whom he got at least part of his biting humor, eccentric relatives, dogs, of course dogs, school and military training, ghosts, burglars, the day the dam broke and yet didn't and of course his difficulties with his fading vision. So few words, but they say it all and they say it with humor. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6485365497806575250" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1058114434632701758">I remember having to read "The Dog That Bit People" in class during my sophomore year of high school. Twenty five bowed heads in a room, each making no sound (save the occasional sigh), and one nerd giggling his bespectacled head off, which was me<br/><br/>These stories are absolutely superb. "The Night the Ghost Got In" and "The Dog That Bit People" are wonderful, and the episode in "More Alarms At Night" where his dad "threatened to get Buck" is laugh-out-loud hilarious. I love the characters, as well. </span>
  <span id="freeText1058114434632701758" style="display:none">I remember having to read "The Dog That Bit People" in class during my sophomore year of high school. Twenty five bowed heads in a room, each making no sound (save the occasional sigh), and one nerd giggling his bespectacled head off, which was me<br /><br />These stories are absolutely superb. "The Night the Ghost Got In" and "The Dog That Bit People" are wonderful, and the episode in "More Alarms At Night" where his dad "threatened to get Buck" is laugh-out-loud hilarious. I love the characters, as well. Thurber's grandfather, a Civil War veteran, steals the book, and the Get-Ready Man is one of my favorites.<br /><br />I don't know what else to say about this book, except that I love it. <br /></span>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12943954615289254837">I find the disparaging comments from twenty-somethings about Thurber's "My Life and Hard Times" amusing. So would Thurber, because he could always use another philistine foil for his smart protagonist. If you find this material to be too dated you should get off Facebook long enough to have real interactions with real people, then you'd appreciate Thurber's wickedly understated and subtle wit. If you don't get it, it's not because the material isn't funny. It's because you don't get it. Get it?</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/143042494" />
    <a title="Huma Rashid" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2845065-huma-rashid"><img alt="Huma Rashid" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1328728059p2/2845065.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/143042494?book_show_action=true">Jan 23, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Huma Rashid" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Huma Rashid" href="/user/show/2845065-huma-rashid">Huma Rashid</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2845065-huma-rashid?shelf=classic">classic</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer143042494" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15056484770334422818">I read this book for the first time shortly after moving to the Midwest. I moved here from Boston when I was ten, and a year or two later, I was flipping through my anthology textbook for my Literature class and found a short story written by James Thurber. It was absolutely hilarious, so I went to the library and checked to see if he had written any actual books. This popped right up so I borrowed it and took it home to read.<br/><br/>This short 'autobiography' is dry, witty, self-deprecating and interes</span>
  <span id="freeText15056484770334422818" style="display:none">I read this book for the first time shortly after moving to the Midwest. I moved here from Boston when I was ten, and a year or two later, I was flipping through my anthology textbook for my Literature class and found a short story written by James Thurber. It was absolutely hilarious, so I went to the library and checked to see if he had written any actual books. This popped right up so I borrowed it and took it home to read.<br /><br />This short 'autobiography' is dry, witty, self-deprecating and interesting, but for me, it was a lesson in how to be Midwestern. I became much more firm in my Midwesternity after reading this. It's an odd thing to say, and I realize this, but you really do have to read this book to understand what I mean, and once you do, you will. You really, really will. This book oozes Midwestern values and perspectives. It's absolutely undeniable. <br /><br />I'd recommend it to anyone looking for something light and fun, but still serious in a self-deprecating, exasperated way. It'd make a great airplane read; you'd probably be able to finish it on a flight from DC to Chicago. You won't learn any axioms about the universe, you won't feel emotionally purged, and you probably won't be on the floor laughing, but you'll take something else away from it, I'm sure. I think it has to be different for everyone. After all, this is just a man telling stories about his family, which is what makes this book so pleasant, irritating, and fun to read. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15056484770334422818" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_272268306" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/272268306" />
    <a title="Emily" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7618715-emily"><img alt="Emily" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1331135138p2/7618715.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/272268306?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emily" href="/user/show/7618715-emily">Emily</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7618715-emily?shelf=favorite-kid-s-books">favorite-kid-s-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer272268306" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14689303199505486813">Sadly, this wonderful book is hardly ever referred to. While the world knows that Charles Dickens, Ernest Hemingway, and the Bronte sisters wrote Literature, hardly anyone who hasn't read Thurber recognizes the name. And he's got to be very near the best humor writer out there. I can drive my family crazy by giggling out loud while I'm reading him. They complain, and ask me to stop, but I just can't. I am incapable of reading this collection (his best, in my opinion) out loud, because I burst ou</span>
  <span id="freeText14689303199505486813" style="display:none">Sadly, this wonderful book is hardly ever referred to. While the world knows that Charles Dickens, Ernest Hemingway, and the Bronte sisters wrote Literature, hardly anyone who hasn't read Thurber recognizes the name. And he's got to be very near the best humor writer out there. I can drive my family crazy by giggling out loud while I'm reading him. They complain, and ask me to stop, but I just can't. I am incapable of reading this collection (his best, in my opinion) out loud, because I burst out laughing at inopportune moments. Bill Bryson has nothing on James Thurber. <i>P. G. Wodehouse</i> has nothing on Thurber. And this collection in particular, containing, as it does, such classics as "The Night the Bed Fell" and "The Day the Dam Broke," is just spectacular. It's clear, concise writing at its best, and is responsible for haunting me with the story of his grandfather, horse-breaker extraordinaire, trying to break the car to his will, and escaping with it to the park one day, where they came across him, driving it in tight circles and trying to bend it to his will.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14689303199505486813" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_100813789" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/100813789" />
    <a title="Sarah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3124054-sarah"><img alt="Sarah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1331096191p2/3124054.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/100813789?book_show_action=true">Apr 30, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah" href="/user/show/3124054-sarah">Sarah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer100813789" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5315529418796115456">My Life and Hard Times. James Thurber. 1933. Perennial Classics. 106 pages. ISBN 0060933089.<br/><br/>Okay, okay, so I know James Thurber is a celebrated author and artist who spent the majority of his career writing for The New Yorker, but that was over 50 years ago. I really need to start washing my hands of classics such as these because they're just too old. I can appreciate his talent, but from an enjoyment standpoint I just need to stick to later, humorous biographies written by people that are stil</span>
  <span id="freeText5315529418796115456" style="display:none">My Life and Hard Times. James Thurber. 1933. Perennial Classics. 106 pages. ISBN 0060933089.<br /><br />Okay, okay, so I know James Thurber is a celebrated author and artist who spent the majority of his career writing for The New Yorker, but that was over 50 years ago. I really need to start washing my hands of classics such as these because they're just too old. I can appreciate his talent, but from an enjoyment standpoint I just need to stick to later, humorous biographies written by people that are still alive and dwelling in current times. Even reading Garrison Keillor's Lake Wobegon Days is pushing it.<br /><br />My Life and Hard Times is a very short, little book telling funny little stories from James Thurber's childhood, teenage, and college years. I have heard these types of "when I was young we walked 20 miles up the hill to school in snow" stories BILLIONS of times, so although this book may have been original at some point in time, it definitely isn't for me. My Life and Hard Times is not terrible or poorly written, but is however fairly boring and made me sleepy.<br /><br />My favorite chapter is "University Days". Thurber tells of his frustration in science class trying to get the microscope to work without any luck. His rant about seeing dust particles or seeing his own eyeball is hilarious because I can totally relate! <br /><br />Although the book is only 106 pages long, I would definitely recommend picking up a more modern humorous biography if you're just looking for light and funny entertainment. David Sedaris is quite prolific in the humorous memoir world, and many successful comedians such as Sarah Silverman and Wanda Sykes have their own books as well. <br /><br />Read more book reviews by DreamSE22 at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://DreamworldBooks.com">http://DreamworldBooks.com</a>!</span>
  <a data-text-id="5315529418796115456" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_388440743" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/388440743" />
    <a title="Realini" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7873218-realini"><img alt="Realini" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420269105p2/7873218.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/388440743?book_show_action=true">Aug 10, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Realini" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Realini" href="/user/show/7873218-realini">Realini</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer388440743" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7078116862971018340">Thurber and the Wide Sargasso Sea<br/>As I try to write about Thurber, with his My Life and Hard Times and The Wide Sargasso sea by Jean Rhys, I think that I should change my reading strategy.<br/><br/>At this stage, I try to read all that the great books on the top 100-150 lists of books given by The Modern Library, TIME, The Guardian, Friendswood and eventually Le Monde. Le Monde has a different perspective, with its list of best books- „Starting from a preliminary list of 200 titles created by bookshops and</span>
  <span id="freeText7078116862971018340" style="display:none">Thurber and the Wide Sargasso Sea<br />As I try to write about Thurber, with his My Life and Hard Times and The Wide Sargasso sea by Jean Rhys, I think that I should change my reading strategy.<br /><br />At this stage, I try to read all that the great books on the top 100-150 lists of books given by The Modern Library, TIME, The Guardian, Friendswood and eventually Le Monde. Le Monde has a different perspective, with its list of best books- „Starting from a preliminary list of 200 titles created by bookshops and journalists, 17,000 French voted by responding to the question, "Which books have stayed in your memory?" (« Quels livres sont restés dans votre mémoire ? »).[“ <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Le_Monde%27s_100_Books_of_the_Century">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Le_Monde...</a><br /><br />I found the Le Monde list only recently, appreciate it as very interesting – some of the very first books on it: L’etranger, A la recherche du Temps Perdu, Voyage au bout de la Nuit, The Grapes of Wrath are at the very top on my own list. To make me even happier, I have read them and am ready to read again (and again), after I go through the other lists. Or maybe I will not?<br /> I should read more humor? Some of the books that I set myself to read (from the above mentioned resources) have been a joy, others a mixed blessing and in a few cases a total fiasco-for me, I am not a fool (I hope) so I do not pass judgement-indeed, I continue to believe in the advise of experts, critics who recommend those books- I am mentioning in my notes what I liked, what sent my imagination flying, but elsewhere, not along with the plot of the book. This is just my experience, my emotional rescue ...<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=FR3r0z3_oig">http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=FR3r0z...</a><br />„I come to your emotional rescue”, The Rolling Stones<br /><br />This is a question mark: why not read those books that give the Maximum pleasure, like Thurber’s My life and Hard Times...<br /><br />There is, after all the Guardian list of 1,000 books to read, with a special section for Comedy books-why not look for more pleasure there? <br />Instead of finding likeable passages in the Wide Sargasso Sea, perhaps I should stick to the literature which causes the Biggest bang?<br />The Wide Sargasso Sea is a book I enjoyed, but if I put it in the balance with My Life and Hard Times, the latter wins hands down. I would take the Thurber on the famous desrted island, if the question ever arises.<br /><br />I did have empathy for the characters of Jean Rhys, speaking of island, the action takes place on carribean islands, in the Wide Sargasso Sea, as opposed to Thurber, where chaos descends on a plain house, the attic where „The „Night The Bed Fell on My Father”-that’s the name of a chapter in Thurber’s book.<br /><br />In the Wide Sargasso Sea there is violence,a parrot suffers in the book and I hated that, as the owner of a stupendous Blue and Gold Macaw, near me now...<br /><br />There is the stupidity and cruelty of native people of the islands, but they could very well have been chimpanzees, for the IQ they prove in their actions.<br /><br />There is misycism, Voo Doo and belief in all kinds of ghosts, who show up and demons.<br /><br />It all ends tragically, at least that’s my perception. Gone should be for me the preconceived idea of peacefull islands, pure images of terrestrial Paradise. The Blue Calm Sea of the Carribeans should make way, after reading this book, to an image of reality (?) which shocks- with poverty, ignorance, violence and exotique beliefs...<br /><br />There is much to read about in the Wide Sargasso Sea, it is for good reason that critics appreciate it, here the question for me is to estimate which way my reading plans should look forward.<br /><br />In the background there is another pressure to reconsider what I (and others?) should read. There is a lonng line of „Celebrity Thinkers”who parade their Wisdom, on romanian Tv, speaking most of them in favor of the suspended president. If studying Hegel, Kant, Heiddeger brings one to the level of inteligence where on accepts a rude, abusive „Chef d’etat” then it might be all in vain and one could do as well, or better, with Evelyn Waugh, included otherwise in the lists of the Great and Famous, and his Scoop.<br /><br />How can this be? I get the argument of Gavril the Clever, about the USL mistakes, abuses, misdemenours, outrageous interference and much more...but to support a fucked up idol, who forced down the throught of his party and country the illeterate daughter- sent to represent us in the European Parliament ...this is beyond good, common sense, it gives the feeling that ivory tower high reading gets you „High”with the same effect „weed”would have...<br /><br />On the other hand, I am reading Philip Roth – Portnoy’s Complaint, an established, recognized award winning author. I was amazed by the first part: fabulous, funny to the Max, outrageous, sexy...<br /><br />But too much sex is already getting on my nerves, i am no Augustine, so it might be that his kinky stuff is simply not my kind of erotic paraphernalia...I remember one of the mobsters in Good Fellas or The Casino I guess, who was against mixing stuff you do with your wife (more „decent”) and things you can do with the mistress (wilder).<br /><br />Maybe it’s about the same thing: too many erotique posititions, pervetions are mixed up with the mother, the lover the would-be wife.<br /><br />I am only half way through and I’ll get back to you on this one... <br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7078116862971018340" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1082482861" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1082482861" />
    <a title="Laura Verret" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2511187-laura-verret"><img alt="Laura Verret" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1523559024p2/2511187.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1082482861?book_show_action=true">Oct 17, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laura Verret" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laura Verret" href="/user/show/2511187-laura-verret">Laura Verret</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2511187-laura-verret?shelf=biographies-auto-biographies">biographies-auto-biographies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2511187-laura-verret?shelf=classic-fiction">classic-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2511187-laura-verret?shelf=goodwill-finds">goodwill-finds</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1082482861" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer909545359179092950">When I was in ninth grade, I read a book called <i>Themes of Literature</i> as my literature program for the year. (Of course, I read gobs of other books, too.) There were many stories in that book that I’ve already forgotten – that I forgot within weeks of reading them. But then there were others that stuck with me…<br/><br/>I’ve mentioned the chapter from <i>Call It Courage</i> which I found in that book and which made me long to read the rest of the story. And I’ve mentioned reading A. A. Milne’s play <i>The Ugly Duckl</i></span>
  <span id="freeText909545359179092950" style="display:none">When I was in ninth grade, I read a book called <i>Themes of Literature</i> as my literature program for the year. (Of course, I read gobs of other books, too.) There were many stories in that book that I’ve already forgotten – that I forgot within weeks of reading them. But then there were others that stuck with me…<br /><br />I’ve mentioned the chapter from <i>Call It Courage</i> which I found in that book and which made me long to read the rest of the story. And I’ve mentioned reading A. A. Milne’s play <i>The Ugly Duckling</i> in it. There were only two other pieces which stuck with me – a short story about a soldier’s homecoming and <i>The Night the Bed Fell</i> which happens to have come from this collection of short stories by James Thurber.<br /><br />Seriously, everyone needs to read <i>The Night the Bed Fell</i> at least three times. It’s just that good. It follows a path of escalating incidents in which everyone concerned is convinced that either they or someone else is dying, and arrives at a hilarious denoument. Now, having read the entire book, I know that his <i>The Dog That Bit People</i> and <i>More Alarms at Night</i> follow similarly and equally hilarious lines.<br /><br />James Thurber’s stories are based upon his own experiences and so reflect not merely the funny, but also the sad and regrettable. His stories have comedic elements to them, but they are <i>not</i> comedy. They are wholly humorous, but humor is not their goal. They are primarily satire – gentle satire, certainly, but satire none the less. They poke fun at everything and everyone and with good reason. His childhood was filled with numerous eccentric and unforgettable characters, such as his grandfather, who was convinced that the Civil War was still being fought, his aunt, who believed that her home had been burglarized every night for forty years, and the hordes of housemaids which circulated throughout his home (especially the one who shot, not only the gas lamps over the fireplace, but also a man).<br /><br />I’ll give you an example of the writing style just by including a few of the story openers for you. :)<br /><br /><br />“<i>I suppose that the high-water mark of my youth in Columbus, Ohio, was the night the bed fell on my father. It makes a better recitation (unless, as some friends of mine have said, one has heard it five or six times) than it does a piece of writing, for it is almost necessary to throw furniture around, shake doors, and bark like a dog, to lend the proper atmosphere and verisimilitude to what is admittedly a somewhat incredible tale. Still, it did take place</i>.” [pg. 3]<br /><br /><br />Even without the throwing of furniture, the story is wizard. :D<br /><br /><br />“<i>The ghost that got into our house on the night of November 17, 1915, raised such a hullabloo of misunderstanding that I am sorry I didn’t just let it keep on walking, and go to bed. Its adventure caused my mother to throw a shoe through a window of the house next door and ended up with my grandfather shooting a patrolman. I am sorry, therefore, as I have said, that I ever paid any attention to the footsteps</i>." [pg. 32]<br /><br /><br />After James notices the ‘ghost’, his mother decides to call the police.<br /><br /><br />“<i>Since the phone was downstairs, I didn’t see how we were going to call the police – nor did I want the police – but mother made one of her quick, incomparable decisions. She flung up a window of her bedroom which faced the bedroom windows of the house of a neighbor, picked up a shoe, and whammed it through a pane of glass across the narrow space that separated the two houses. Glass tinkled into the bedroom occupied by a retired engraver named Bodwell and his wife. Bodwell had been for some years in rather a bad way and was subject to mild “attacks.” Most everybody we knew or lived near had some kind of attacks.<br /><br /> It was now about two o’clock of a moonless night; clouds hung black and low. Bodwell was at the window in a minute, shouting, frothing a little, shaking his fist. “We’ll sell the house and go back to Peoria,” we could hear Mrs. Bodwell saying. It was some time before mother “got through” to Bodwell. “Burglars!” she shouted. “Burglars in the house!” Herman and I hadn’t dared to tell her that it was not burglars but ghosts, for she was even more afraid of ghosts than of burglars. Bodwell at first thought that she meant there were burglars in his house, but finally he quieted down and called the police for us over an extension phone by his bed. After he had disappeared from the window, mother suddenly made as if to throw another shoe, not because there was further need of it but, as she later explained, because the thrill of heaving a shoe through a window glass had enormously taken her fancy. I prevented her.” </i>[pg. 35]<br /><br />:) :) :)<br /><br />I honestly felt that I would have enjoyed the stories more had they not been decorated with the author’s own illustrations. The stories themselves had an incisive wit about them, but the lumpy-looking drawings really took away from their sharpness.<br /><br /><b>Conclusion.</b> James Thurber was a wildly talented and wildly unknown author from the twentieth century. Keep an eye out for his stuff.<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.blitheringbookster.com">www.blitheringbookster.com</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="909545359179092950" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2600760320" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2600760320" />
    <a title="Mary" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1460812-mary"><img alt="Mary" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1339640891p2/1460812.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2600760320?book_show_action=true">Nov 28, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mary" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mary" href="/user/show/1460812-mary">Mary</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2600760320" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6663537926642255021">Yes, it is somewhat dated; Thurber's fluid prose isn't what some young people are used to reading today. Worse, I can see modern readers being offended by the sketch of Vashti and her boyfriend. Since Thurber skewers the white maids, and, indeed, many of his family members just as thoroughly, I can accept Vashti. As exaggerated as hers or many of the other situations can seem, somehow it's all absolutely believable. We've seen situations and people like this.<br/><br/>And--I've read this book more than th</span>
  <span id="freeText6663537926642255021" style="display:none">Yes, it is somewhat dated; Thurber's fluid prose isn't what some young people are used to reading today. Worse, I can see modern readers being offended by the sketch of Vashti and her boyfriend. Since Thurber skewers the white maids, and, indeed, many of his family members just as thoroughly, I can accept Vashti. As exaggerated as hers or many of the other situations can seem, somehow it's all absolutely believable. We've seen situations and people like this.<br /><br />And--I've read this book more than three times, and I still smile or laugh every time. What still gets me: Briggs and the camphor. The get-ready man. Thurber's aunt's graceful narration of her experience in the flood. The cars! And, especially, Thurber's experiences in college and the dog who saw ghosts.<br /><br />I think this book would not remain half so funny if Thurber were not, deep down, so serious: so clear-eyed and compassionate about his crazy family and his own foibles. I love the drawings, too. Somehow, they convey the tone and character of the stories perfectly, simple though they are. Not, perhaps, a perfect book, but it's still a gem. If you like Mark Twain and other classic American humor, you will like this.<br /></span>

<div id="review_548662340" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/548662340" />
    <a title="The Book Maven" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/47212-the-book-maven"><img alt="The Book Maven" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1178140714p2/47212.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/548662340?book_show_action=true">Feb 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="The Book Maven" class="user" itemprop="url" name="The Book Maven" href="/user/show/47212-the-book-maven">The Book Maven</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47212-the-book-maven?shelf=read-biography-and-memoir">read-biography-and-memoir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47212-the-book-maven?shelf=read-in-2013">read-in-2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer548662340" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14546890994792622672">When I was in my teens, my father decided I needed a little more culture in my life. So he bought me a subscription to <i>The New Yorker</i>.<br/><br/>If I didn't dislike the old man so much, I’d feel bad about wasting his money. Because me and <i>The New Yorker</i>…we didn't get each other. I tried, I really tried to read and understand the articles. (Even then, I knew ENJOYING them would be beyond my meager abilities.) But it was a hopeless case. My idea of culture was the next Christian Slater movie, or the newest <i>B</i></span>
  <span id="freeText14546890994792622672" style="display:none">When I was in my teens, my father decided I needed a little more culture in my life. So he bought me a subscription to <i>The New Yorker</i>.<br /><br />If I didn't dislike the old man so much, I’d feel bad about wasting his money. Because me and <i>The New Yorker</i>…we didn't get each other. I tried, I really tried to read and understand the articles. (Even then, I knew ENJOYING them would be beyond my meager abilities.) But it was a hopeless case. My idea of culture was the next Christian Slater movie, or the newest <i>Babysitter’s Club</i> book.<br /><br />So all these years later, it shouldn't be a surprise that I didn't enjoy Thurber’s little autobiography. While my culture quotient has gone up, and I can appreciate a smart joke, I simply fail to see what was amusing about the various anecdotes about Thurber’s childhood. Most of the stories revolved around some misunderstanding or comedy of errors, and while the situations themselves may have been amusing, his recounting of them was not. <br /><br />Plus, he makes everyone in his hometown of Columbus, Ohio—and by association, Midwesterners in general—seem like a bunch of foolish yokels. To me, that’s pretty close to an unforgivable sin, and not something I enjoy in a book. <br /><br />I get that he was a writer and artist for <i>The New Yorker</i>, which probably explains why I didn't dig it. I’m certain most of my dislike for this book is simply a matter of my own taste (or lack thereof), so read and enjoy. And if you don’t like it, don’t worry too much. It’s a fast read. I think it took me longer to write the book review than it did for me to read the book!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14546890994792622672" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_618545479" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/618545479" />
    <a title="Ellen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3796359-ellen"><img alt="Ellen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1303763029p2/3796359.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/618545479?book_show_action=true">May 18, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ellen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ellen" href="/user/show/3796359-ellen">Ellen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/17932636-my-life-and-hard-times">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer618545479" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10270838440456896310">What a fun and fast read this one is! Of course, it's really dated, but if you don't mind that Thurber's sense of humor just keeps you moving on through these very funny stories of his family life. You can just see the characters in action - the father having the bed fall on him, the little brother acting up, the mother being a crazy woman, and more. Very funny!<br/><br/>I'd recommend this one to anyone who likes humor, and who enjoys James Thurber. I'm moving on to the next one of his books that I found </span>
  <span id="freeText10270838440456896310" style="display:none">What a fun and fast read this one is! Of course, it's really dated, but if you don't mind that Thurber's sense of humor just keeps you moving on through these very funny stories of his family life. You can just see the characters in action - the father having the bed fall on him, the little brother acting up, the mother being a crazy woman, and more. Very funny!<br /><br />I'd recommend this one to anyone who likes humor, and who enjoys James Thurber. I'm moving on to the next one of his books that I found at a wonderful used book store in Galena, Illinois. This one is about Thurber's time on the staff of the New York, with the editor being the main focus of attention.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10270838440456896310" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer58999524" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4411005903328553496">I actually have the fifth printing of the 1961 Bantam Classic edition, but I don't know how to get that information into the system if it's not already there.<br/>James Thurber has a gift with words. He always knows when to add a particular detail and when to add a comment on the events he's narrating. This collection of stories leads me to believe that the author grew up in a uproariously crazy household; in fact, he could have lived a commonplace life, but his telling of it is exquisite.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2339547932" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2339547932" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2339547932?book_show_action=true">Mar 25, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="William" class="user" itemprop="url" name="William" href="/user/show/3082189-william">William</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2339547932" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13697042507599738093">Let's be clear - this is a book from another age. You can smell the horses in the street and hear the tobacco juice hitting the spittoons, when peoples' height was compared to standing corn. It has dialect humor with African-American servants in one brief passage. But it is an entertaining exhibition of style with great sentences on every page. "He gazed at me a long time as if I were a slot machine into which he had, without results, dropped a nickel." Wonderful, and very funny.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2560899433" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6968999490267437313">His aunt threw shoes down the stairs every night to scare potential burglars. His mother told her sons they better not “drive all over town with no gasoline.” His father had nightmares that he was being chased by President Cleveland. The best humor comes from real life. James Thurber’s memoir about young adulthood in the early 1900s Ohio made me laugh out loud several times.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

           <span class="uitext reviewText">lovers of lit humor</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30019-allison?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1320638" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17784776159543867151">Brillance comes in small packages. James Thurber packs a lot of funny and absurdity in a tiny book that you should definitely read. </span>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2818616283" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9016895398738145281">Picked this up for a dollar on the clearance shelf in Half-Priced Books, really enjoyed reading these stories of Thurber's family life here in Columbus (visited the house where many of the stories took place on Sunday, it is now the Thurber House Museum, open to the public and also home to a writer in residence every year). As charming as I remember them from when I read them as a youngster. And of course I love his drawings.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3208798886" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4255775797618639998">Thurber scores huge in his autobiography consisting the collection of events from his life that has great amount of humour, supported by his witty writing. He cracks us up just from Preface where he makes fun of his own autobiography by quoting Benvenuto Cellini's words. Overall a quick read with great humour and writing!!</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('BbdPfehcs2VqxiOS750kTUi0UkrXtTsBVkAbXo/iWCaoW8Sn9/7mv4A9jOpuZHQAWMdTkxKMkqYRWbKzBQ3Euw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('zI5LMWwTEjVN89h0mXFSNC4FH0QsO8WpsyhuWC++Av1hYsDrc7FH76cIdwwYiAJ5PnYenekCbA70Mce1pVGeYA==')})
  }

Jill Lepore, best-selling author <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText14512562485712629510\" style=\"display:none\">The Simulmatics Corporation, launched during the Cold War, mined data, targeted voters, manipulated consumers, destabilized politics, and disordered knowledge—decades before Facebook, Google, and Cambridge Analytica. Jill Lepore, best-selling author of These Truths, came across the company’s papers in MIT’s archives and set out to tell this forgotten history, the long-lost backstory to the methods, and the arrogance, of Silicon Valley.\n\n\nFounded in 1959 by some of the nation’s leading social scientists—“the best and the brightest, fatally brilliant, Icaruses with wings of feathers and wax, flying to the sun”—Simulmatics proposed to predict and manipulate the future by way of the computer simulation of human behavior. In summers, with their wives and children in tow, the company’s scientists met on the beach in Long Island under a geodesic, honeycombed dome, where they built a “People Machine” that aimed to model everything from buying a dishwasher to counterinsurgency to casting a vote. Deploying their “People Machine” from New York, Washington, Cambridge, and even Saigon, Simulmatics’ clients included the John F. Kennedy presidential campaign, the New York Times, the Department of Defense, and dozens of major manufacturers: Simulmatics had a hand in everything from political races to the Vietnam War to the Johnson administration’s ill-fated attempt to predict race riots. The company’s collapse was almost as rapid as its ascent, a collapse that involved failed marriages, a suspicious death, and bankruptcy. Exposed for false claims, and even accused of war crimes, it closed its doors in 1970 and all but vanished. Until Lepore came across the records of its remains.\n\n\nThe scientists of Simulmatics believed they had invented “the A-bomb of the social sciences.” They did not predict that it would take decades to detonate, like a long-buried grenade. But, in the early years of the twenty-first century, that bomb did detonate, creating a world in which corporations collect data and model behavior and target messages about the most ordinary of decisions, leaving people all over the world, long before the global pandemic, crushed by feelings of helplessness. This history has a past; If Then is its cautionary tale.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"14512562485712629510\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n At the end of her bestselling memoir Mennonite in a Little Black Dress, Rhoda Janzen had reconnected with her family and her roots, though her future felt uncertain. But when she starts dating a churchgoer, this skeptic begins a surprising journey to faith and love. Rhoda doesn\'t slide back into the dignified simplicity of the Mennonite church. Instead she finds herself hanging with the Pentecostals, who really know how to get down with sparkler pom-poms. Amid the hand waving and hallelujahs Rhoda finds a faith richly practical for life -- just in time for some impressive lady problems, an unexpected romance, and a quirky new family. Does This Church Make Me Look Fat? is for people who have a problem with organized religion, but can\'t quite dismiss the notion of God, and for those who secretly sing hymns in their cars, but prefer a nice mimosa brunch to church. This is the story of what it means to find joy in love, comfort in prayer, and -- incredibly, surprisingly -- faith in a big-hearted God.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"18047515883643648196\" href=\"#\"       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/16839.James_Thurber">About James Thurber</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/16839.James_Thurber"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1183238729p3/16839.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/16839.James_Thurber">James Thurber
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
457
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer13240858535451787633">Thurber was born in Columbus, Ohio to Charles L. Thurber and Mary Agnes (Mame) Fisher Thurber. Both of his parents greatly influenced his work. His father, a sporadically employed clerk and minor politician who dreamed of being a lawyer or an actor, is said to have been the inspiration for the small, timid protagonist typical of many of his stories. Thurber described his mother as a "born comedien</span>
  <span id="freeText13240858535451787633" style="display:none">Thurber was born in Columbus, Ohio to Charles L. Thurber and Mary Agnes (Mame) Fisher Thurber. Both of his parents greatly influenced his work. His father, a sporadically employed clerk and minor politician who dreamed of being a lawyer or an actor, is said to have been the inspiration for the small, timid protagonist typical of many of his stories. Thurber described his mother as a "born comedienne" and "one of the finest comic talents I think I have ever known." She was a practical joker, on one occasion pretending to be crippled and attending a faith healer revival, only to jump up and proclaim herself healed.<br /><br />Thurber had two brothers, William and Robert. Once, while playing a game of William Tell, his brother William shot James in the eye with an arrow. Because of the lack of medical technology, Thurber lost his eye. This injury would later cause him to be almost entirely blind. During his childhood he was unable to participate in sports and activities because of his injury, and instead developed a creative imagination, which he shared in his writings.<br /><br />From 1913 to 1918, Thurber attended The Ohio State University, where he was a member of the Phi Kappa Psi Fraternity. He never graduated from the University because his poor eyesight prevented him from taking a mandatory ROTC course. In 1995 he was posthumously awarded a degree.<br /><br />From 1918 to 1920, at the close of World War I, Thurber worked as a code clerk for the Department of State, first in Washington, D.C. and then at the American Embassy in Paris, France. After this Thurber returned to Columbus, where he began his writing career as a reporter for the Columbus Dispatch from 1921 to 1924. During part of this time, he reviewed current books, films, and plays in a weekly column called "Credos and Curios," a title that later would be given to a posthumous collection of his work. Thurber also returned to Paris in this period, where he wrote for the Chicago Tribune and other newspapers.<br /><br />In 1925, he moved to Greenwich Village in New York City, getting a job as a reporter for the New York Evening Post. He joined the staff of The New Yorker in 1927 as an editor with the help of his friend and fellow New Yorker contributor, E.B. White. His career as a cartoonist began in 1930 when White found some of Thurber's drawings in a trash can and submitted them for publication. Thurber would contribute both his writings and his drawings to The New Yorker until the 1950s.<br /><br />Thurber was married twice. In 1922, Thurber married Althea Adams. The marriage was troubled and ended in divorce in May 1935. Adams gave Thurber his only child, his daughter Rosemary. Thurber remarried in June, 1935 to Helen Wismer. His second marriage lasted until he died in 1961, at the age of 66, due to complications from pneumonia, which followed upon a stroke suffered at his home. His last words, aside from the repeated word "God," were "God bless... God damn," according to Helen Thurber.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13240858535451787633" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
